    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="ab33af92e3f5476c99802e40557b4a36">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: 'c1c28acc-0da4-481b-b0cf-9aca2bdd9c1d'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

                        <li class="sm-account__item"><a target="_blank" class="sm-account__link" href="https://orcid.org/0000-0001-9206-3719"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-orcid"></i><span>orcid.org/0000-0001-9206-3719</span></a></li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>Chemical Science and Engineering Division, Argonne National Laboratory, Lemont, IL, 60439 USA</p>
                     <p>E-mail: <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:liut@anl.gov"><span>liut@anl.gov</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:amine@anl.gov"><span>amine@anl.gov</span></a>; <a class="corr-email" title="Link to email address" href="mailto:hsli@qdu.edu.cn"><span>hsli@qdu.edu.cn</span></a></p><a class="moreInfoLink" href="/authored-by/Amine/Khalil">Search for more papers by this author</a></div></span><span class="accordion-tabbed__tab-mobile  accordion__closed"><a href="/authored-by/Li/Hongsen" class="author-name accordion-tabbed__control" data-id="a8" data-db-target-for="a8" aria-controls="a8" aria-haspopup="true" id="a8_Ctrl" role="button"><span>Hongsen Li<i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-mail_outline"></i></span><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-section_arrow_d"></i></a><span class="comma-separator">,&nbsp;</span><div class="author-info accordion-tabbed__content" data-db-target-of="a8" aria-labelledby="a8_Ctrl" role="region" id="a8">

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d111763540" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>The P2-type layered transition metal oxide cathodes confront formidable challenges, including irreversible deleterious phase transitions, transition metals migration, and sluggish Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, which hamper their rapid commercial application in sodium ion batteries (SIB). In this work, an entropy tuning with dual-site substitution strategy is proposed to address the aforementioned issues. In the tailored [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NZNCMTO) cathodes, the strategic incorporation of Zn ions serves to occupy Na sites, intentionally disrupting the Na/vacancy ordering and establishing a reinforcing “pillar” effect within the layered framework. Furthermore, the substitution of Cu and Ti for Ni and Mn bolsters covalent bonding with the lattice oxygen, thereby impeding the migration of the transition metal ions and leading to a near-zero strain structural evolution during charge and discharge process. Density functional theory calculations confirmed that entropy-tuned NZNCMTO substantially lowered the migration energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> ions diffusion and improved electronic conductivity. Consequently, the NZNCMTO cathode exhibits an impressive high practical capacity of 91.54 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at a high discharge rate of 10 C, along with outstanding cycling stability, maintaining near 100% capacity retention over 500 cycles at a current density of 10 C. This work presents an innovative blueprint for designing high-performance sodium-ion battery cathode materials.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315437-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Recently, the rapid growth in demand for renewable energy storage has fueled further development of rechargeable batteries. However, the scarcity of lithium resources may hinder the sustainable development of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), which have dominated the market for a long time.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0001_R_d111763398e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nature.">1</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0005_R_d111763398e485" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem.-Int. Edit.">5</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, many researchers are studying rechargeable batteries like potassium,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0006_R_d111763398e490" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> calcium,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0007_R_d111763398e495" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Small.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> sodium,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0008_R_d111763398e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">8</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0011_R_d111763398e501" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Sci. China Chem.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and aluminum-ion batteries<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0012_R_d111763398e506" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Energy Environ. Sci.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> as potential replacements for LIBs, to cater to the demand of large-scale energy storage applications. Sodium batteries (SIBs) have attracted significant attention as a low-cost alternative to lithium systems due to the abundance of Na resources and the similar electrochemical properties between sodium and lithium. Among a range of cathodes, layered P2-type Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) has emerged as a potential cathode for SIBs owing to its open framework, high theoretical capacity, and facile synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0013_R_d111763398e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Energy.">13</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0016_R_d111763398e520" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, it has been confirmed that the cathodes exhibit oxygen anion redox when charging above 4.0&nbsp;V (vs Na/Na<sup>+</sup>),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0017_R_d111763398e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Commun.">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0018_R_d111763398e528" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> leading to a capacity increase of more than 200 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>. However, the structure of P2-type NNMO is unstable mainly because of the detrimental phase transition of P2-OP4/-O2 with the pronounced volume variation at deep desodiation state caused by the gliding of transition metal slabs and MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedral structure distortion at approximately the end of discharge due to the Jahn-Teller effect of Mn<sup>3+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0013_R_d111763398e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - Nano Energy.">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0015_R_d111763398e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Energy Storage Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0016_R_d111763398e540" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">16</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, while oxygen redox provides additional capacity, it can also result in the formation of undercoordinated oxygen and non-bonded O 2p states, which can lead to detrimental cationic migration and lattice oxygen distortion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0019_R_d111763398e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0021_R_d111763398e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Consequently, improving the stability of the phase and stabilizing the irreversible oxygen reaction are urgent tasks in the development of P2-type Mn-rich cathodes with oxygen redox to enhance cycle life and energy density.</p>
         
         <p>Extensive research in the past decades has primarily focused on stabilizing the main structure and anionic redox in P2-type Mn-based layered cathodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0022_R_d111763398e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Sci. (Weinh).">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0024_R_d111763398e553" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Small.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> One effective approach to achieve this is by introducing electrochemically active transition metals (such as Ni, Co, or Fe) in TM layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0025_R_d111763398e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - J. Energy Chem.">25</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0027_R_d111763398e558" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Energy Storage Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These metals can transfer charge compensation centers and mitigate the Jahn-Teller distortion of Mn<sup>3+</sup>. Moreover, doping with electrochemical inert elements (including Zn, Mg, or Ca) plays a significant role in stabilizing the crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0019_R_d111763398e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Adv. Mater.">19</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0020_R_d111763398e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0028_R_d111763398e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0029_R_d111763398e566" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Another aspect that contributes to the stability of the cathode material is the application of a stable coating on its surface.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0030_R_d111763398e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Alloy. Compd.">30</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0035_R_d111763398e571" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Angew. Chem.-Int. Edit.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This coating effectively protects the electrode material from electrolyte corrosion and reduces side reactions, ultimately leading to a better cycle life. Nevertheless, it is important to note that coating with inactive substances presents a double-edged sword. Although it provides additional stability, it may also reduce the energy density of the electrode material.</p>
         
         <p>High-entropy oxides have recently received significant attention due to their impressive structural stability during charge/discharge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0036_R_d111763398e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Nat. Mater.">36</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0041_R_d111763398e579" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Angew. Chem.-Int. Edit.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Hu et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0038_R_d111763398e584" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed. Engl.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> reported that the high-entropy O3-type NaNi<sub>0.12</sub>Cu<sub>0.12</sub>Mg<sub>0.12</sub>Fe<sub>0.15</sub>Co<sub>0.15</sub>Mn<sub>0.1</sub>Ti<sub>0.1</sub>Sn<sub>0.1</sub>Sb<sub>0.04</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode could enable reversible cationic redox within a voltage range of 2.0–3.9&nbsp;V while suppressing the O3-P3 phase transition.</p>
         
         <p>A crucial role in this context is also played by metal ions (such as Zn, Mg, or Ca) that fill the sodium vacancies in the alkali metal layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0028_R_d111763398e614" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This filling serves to widen the diffusion channel for sodium ions and inhibit sodium/vacancy order, which is closely related to rate capability. It is worth noting that this particular strategy has not been reported in high-entropy material systems before.</p>
         
         <p>In this work, P2-type [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NZNCMTO) cathode materials were designed and synthesized using entropy tuning and dual-site substitution strategies. The designed materials have Zn occupying the Na site, as confirmed by experimental and theoretical calculations. The entropy-tuned NZNCMTO cathode exhibits excellent cycling stability and ion diffusion rate, as evidenced by a series of electrochemical performance test comparisons. Additionally, in situ, XRD, XPS, and XAS tests probed the crystal structure evolution and charge compensation mechanism during charging and discharging. The theoretical calculations further demonstrate that the entropy-tuned NZNCMTO cathode possesses outstanding ion and electron diffusion kinetics and crystal thermodynamic stability.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315437-sec-0020-title">2 Result and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202315437-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structural and Morphological Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Entropy-tuned [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NZNCMTO) and other control samples [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NZNCMO), [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NZNMTO), Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.27</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNCMTO), and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) were synthesized by a conventional solid-state reaction. As shown in <b>Figures</b> <a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0001">1a</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information), all the samples are indexed to P2-type structure with a space group of <i>P63/mmc</i> and free of any impurities. And the XRD Rietveld refinement results are shown in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S5</a> (Supporting Information). It is noteworthy that there is no peak of the superstructure in entropy-tuned NZNCMTO (inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0001">1a</a>), which is related to sodium/vacancy ordering,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0039_R_d111763398e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Adv. Energy Mater.">39</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0042_R_d111763398e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nano Energy.">42</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0046_R_d111763398e733" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nat. Mater.">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting that multi-ion doping can eliminate sodium/vacancy ordering. Another possible reason is that Na occupancy (Na<sub>e</sub> and Na<sub>f</sub>) were occupied by Zn ions (0.74 Å) that with a similar radius to Na (1.02 Å) during the formation of the crystal structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0028_R_d111763398e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further illustrate, a reliable evaluation of the material model was performed using Density functional theory (DFT) calculations. The formation energy results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0001">1b</a>) verify that Zn ions tend to reside in the Na layer. Moreover, the energy of Zn extraction from the Na layer is higher than that of the TM layer (6.90&nbsp;eV vs 6.47&nbsp;eV) (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting that Zn ions are more inclined to occupy the Na site for higher thermodynamic stability. Further, XRD Rietveld refinement results show that Zn ions occupy the tetrahedral sites of sodium, which fits well with the theoretical and experimental results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0001">1c</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1a</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315437-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/62cd5d8f-8de9-47cc-92e1-f7ebd4af3d99/adfm202315437-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/62cd5d8f-8de9-47cc-92e1-f7ebd4af3d99/adfm202315437-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b8162b47-efa4-49ab-bf22-4136d2dabb27/adfm202315437-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/62cd5d8f-8de9-47cc-92e1-f7ebd4af3d99/adfm202315437-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315437-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Powder XRD patterns of the prepared materials. The inset shows an enlargement within the 2<i>θ</i> range of 25–30°. b) Formation energy and lattice diagram of Zn ion in Na layer and TM layer. c) Rietveld refinements of the XRD pattern for entropy-tuned NZNCMTO. d) Schematic diagram illustrating the crystal structure changes before and after ion doping. e) EDS mapping of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO sample.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Based on the above analysis, the Zn ion occupies the Na site forming a “pillar” structure which is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0001">1d</a>. Due to the electrostatic shielding effect of Zn ions, the layer spacing d<sub>TM-TM</sub> was narrowed from 5.585 to 5.580 Å. However, the O-Na-O distance was enlarged from the original 3.529 to 3.558 Å. This enlargement is assumed to be helpful to facilitate the rapid transport of Na ions during the charging and discharging process. The inconsistent trend in these changes is most presumably due to the strengthening of the TM-O bonds by the substitution of Cu and Ti ions at the Ni and Mn octahedral sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0043_R_d111763398e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0047_R_d111763398e793" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - J. Energy Chem.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively (Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information). Previous studies have shown that the substitution of Ti for elemental Mn forms more stable Ti─O bonds and leads to more localized electrons around the oxygen ion,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0048_R_d111763398e805" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> which facilitates the weakening of the Mn Jahn-Teller effect and binds unstable lattice oxygen which can form unstable oxygen species (O<sub>2</sub><sup>n−</sup>, 0≤n≤2) in the deeply desodiated state, causing structural damage.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0049_R_d111763398e813" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Nat. Mater.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>The morphology and crystal structures of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO and non-entropy-tuned NNMO were characterized using scanning electron microscopy (SEM) and transmission electron microscopy (TEM). The SEM images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) exhibit the typical and well-proportioned lamellar shape of the resulting crystals, which are P2-type layered oxides. Energy-dispersive spectroscopy (EDS) mappings (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0001">1e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2</a>, Supporting Information) displayed a homogeneous distribution of Na, Zn, Ni, Cu, Mn, Ti, and O elements throughout the NZNCMTO particles, and Na, Ni, and Mn elements in the NNMO samples. At the same time, inductively coupled plasma−optical emission spectroscopy (ICP-OES) is taken to confirm the existence of these elements and that the contents are in accordance with the expected settings (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) clearly show well-defined lattice fringes with crystal face spacings of 5.6 and 2.8 Å, corresponding to the (002) and (004) faces of a typical P2-type structure. All the detailed evidence suggests that the target material of the study was successfully synthesized.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202315437-sec-0040-title">2.2 Electrochemical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>To clarify the effectiveness of configurational entropy and dual-site substitution on the structural stability and electrochemical performance of the prepared P2-type cathode materials, a range of electrochemical characterizations were conducted by assembling Na half cells in the voltage range of 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V. <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0002">2a,b</a> shows the electrochemical curves of the NZNCMTO electrode and the NNMO electrode for 10 cycles at 0.1 C (1 C = 170&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), respectively. Compared to the NNMO electrode, which experiences predictable capacity decay and voltage degradation due to the migration of Ni ions, the Jahn-Teller effect, and the deleterious phase transition of P2-OP4/O2, the entropy-tuned NZNCMTO electrode displays a highly overlapping charge/discharge curve and negligible capacity variance. Additionally, the multiple charging and discharging plateaus caused by Na/vacancy ordering and phase transition in the NNMO electrode are greatly alleviated in the NZNCMTO electrode which exhibits smooth curves, resembling a solid solution reaction with fewer phase transitions. Notably, as the electrochemical cycling progresses, the plateau of the high-voltage region (4.0–4.3&nbsp;V) undergoes significant changes between the two electrodes, where the charge compensation is primarily attributed to the oxygen redox (O<sup>2−</sup>/O<sub>2</sub><sup>n−</sup>, 0≤n≤2). The capacity platforms of the NNMO electrode in the high-voltage region disappear rapidly at 10 cycles. In contrast, the NZNCMTO electrode exhibits excellent platform stability in the high-voltage region, indicating the significant impact of the high entropy effect.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315437-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3d4e0864-7ed4-4218-891e-c4b85d2036e9/adfm202315437-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3d4e0864-7ed4-4218-891e-c4b85d2036e9/adfm202315437-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/83654e23-8a91-4cb1-abae-4ec287e091a0/adfm202315437-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3d4e0864-7ed4-4218-891e-c4b85d2036e9/adfm202315437-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315437-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The charge and discharge curves of a) entropy-tuned NZNCMTO and (b) NNMO at 0.1 C. (c) Comparing cycling performances of the two electrodes at 0.1 C. (d) Rate performances. (e) Comparison of the rate properties of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO with the previously reported P2-type layered oxide materials. (f,g) Long-term cycling performances at 1 C and 10 C, respectively. (h) GITT measurement of NZNCMTO at 0.1 C during the second cycle and corresponding Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion coefficients at different charged/discharged states. Insets show the selected steps from the GITT profiles during charge and discharge.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Excellent long-time cycling stability with limited specific capacity decay and superior rate performance can also be achieved for the prepared NZNCMTO. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0002">2c</a> indicates that after 50 cycles at 0.1 C, NZNCMTO maintains a capacity retention rate of 91.2% with an initial discharge capacity of 146.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding midpoint voltage decreases from the initial 3.55 to 3.35&nbsp;V. Whereas the capacity retention rate of NMMO is only 42% with midpoint voltage decreasing from 3.65 to 3.14&nbsp;V, manifesting that Zn ions as a “pillar” structure improve cyclic stability and energy density retention for NZNCMTO. Moreover, NZNCMTO also exhibits capacity retention of 92.7% after exceeding 100 cycles at 1 C and a capacity retention of nearly 100% after exceeding 500 cycles at 10 C (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0002">2f,g</a>). Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0002">2d</a> shows that the rate performance of the NZNCMTO electrode displays discharge capacity of 150.66, 147.37, 140.45, 130.99, 123.53, 110.28, and 91.54 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 0.1, 0.2, 0.5, 1, 2, 5, and 10 C, respectively and when the rate returns to 0.1 C, the discharge capacity can be well recovered. As noted above, the NZNCMTO electrode retains its discharge capacity significantly better than the NNMO electrode at different current densities and exceeds that of P2-type layered oxide materials previously reported (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0002">2e</a>). Furthermore, the diffusion coefficients of Na<sup>+</sup> (D<sub>Na+</sub>) for NZNCMTO and NMMO were measured using the galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) which can explain the excellent performance of the materials. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information) and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0002">2h</a> present the GITT test curve for the two electrodes at 0.1 C in the second cycle. In all potential region, the value of D<sub>Na+</sub> for NZNCMTO is more stable and exceeds that of NMMO. It is particularly noteworthy that D<sub>Na+</sub> evidently decreases in the high-potential region due to slow redox kinetics of anions. Nevertheless, the NZNCMTO electrode exhibits a significant enhancement in D<sub>Na+</sub> value, suggesting the kinetics of anionic redox has a considerable improvement in the entropy tuning and dual-site substitution cathode.</p>
            
            <p>A series of medium entropy materials have been prepared based on NZNCMTO materials to gain a profound understanding of entropy-tuned and dual-site substitution. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) illustrates that the smoothness of the first cycle charge/discharge curve increases as the entropy value rises, indicating that the higher entropy structure facilitates the cathode material in approaching the solid solution reaction during charge/discharge. Furthermore, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> compares the performance of different entropy-value materials in various electrochemical tests. The results clearly show that NZNCMTO materials with highest entropy outperform medium entropy ones in terms of both rate performance and long cycle performance, confirming the effectiveness of the entropy tuning strategy. Within the medium entropy materials, NNCMTO displays the least favorable performance in cycling at 1 C and 10 C, indicating the crucial role of Zn in stabilizing the material structure. Overall, the dual strategy of entropy tuning and dual-site substitution can effectively enhance the electrochemical performance of NNMO.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202315437-sec-0050-title">2.3 Phase Transition and Structural Stability</h3>
            
            <p>The structural stability of the material is closely related to Na<sup>+</sup> kinetics, and phase transitions. The formation of new phases during charging and discharging is usually due to anionic redox, which leads to the creation of oxygen vacancies, the migration of transition metal (TM) ions, and more severe TM layer slip.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0050_R_d111763398e937" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - ACS Nano.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In order to clarify the effects of entropy tuning and dual-site substitution on the structural evolution of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO cathodes, in situ XRD tests were collected during the first cycle (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0003">3a</a>). As charging proceeds, the lattice experienced various changes. The (002) and (004) peaks move to a lower angle due to the increased repulsive electrostatic interactions between neighboring oxygen layers as sodium ions are removed. On the other hand, the (100) and (102) peaks move successively to higher angles, indicating the contraction of the lattice in the a and b directions. It is important to note that the XRD spectra of NZNCMTO demonstrate a completely opposite evolution during discharging. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0003">3b</a>, the characteristic peak (002) corresponding to the P2 phase remains stable and returns to the initial state upon a complete cycle. Furthermore, no new peaks, such as OP4 or O2 phases, appear or vanish during the deep desodiation state (4.3&nbsp;V). This indicates the absence of new phase formation or transition in this material. In contrast, the slip of the transition metal layer and the transition oxidation of oxygen in previously reported NNMO contribute to the generation of the detrimental OP4/O2 phase, resulting in a drastic reduction of the cycle life. Additionally, the refinement results reveal minimal variations in the lattice parameters (a, c, and V) during cycling, with maximum variations of only 1.05%, 1.21%, and 0.78% respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0003">3c</a>). This near-zero strain structural evolution emphasizes the stabilizing effect of entropy tuning and dual-site substitution, providing a structural perspective on the electrochemical stabilization. As depicted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0003">3d</a>, Zn ions remaining in the Na layer act as a support, reducing the repulsion between interlayer oxygen and mitigating lattice stretching in the c-axis direction in the desodiated state. Furthermore, the introduction of Cu and Ti enhances the stability of the TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra due to their stronger bonding interactions with lattice oxygen, consequently increasing the tolerance of lattice distortion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0048_R_d111763398e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0051_R_d111763398e960" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> To further verify the structural stability of NZNCMTO over long cycles, ex situ XRD tests were performed after 100 cycles of charging and discharging (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). The results show that the structure of the material remains highly consistent with the initial state, with no generation of new substances. This further demonstrates the remarkable effectiveness of the entropy tuning and dual-site substitution strategies.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315437-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0ecdd0e1-c1f4-4663-aa96-8ede93136498/adfm202315437-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/0ecdd0e1-c1f4-4663-aa96-8ede93136498/adfm202315437-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8eb9c991-aad2-4cd8-902e-249eced3cd42/adfm202315437-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0ecdd0e1-c1f4-4663-aa96-8ede93136498/adfm202315437-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202315437-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202315437" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) In situ XRD patterns of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO and b) the intensity contour map. c) Evolution of cell parameters and cell volume during the charge/discharge process. d) Schematic diagram of structural evolution during charging of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO. e) Ni f) Mn, and g) Cu K-edge XANES spectra of NZNCMTO collected at different charge/ discharge states.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202315437-sec-0060-title">2.4 Charge Compensation</h3>
            
            <p>The redox reaction and local structural evolution of NZNCMTO during the first charge-discharge process were investigated using X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). In general, the threshold energy position of K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) is sensitive to their oxidation states and can be used to track the chemical states change. As shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0003">3e, g</a>, both the Ni, and Cu <i>K</i>-edge XANES spectra present an obvious shift toward higher energy when charged to 4.3&nbsp;V, indicating the Ni and Cu were oxidized to higher valence states as Na<sup>+</sup> extraction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0052_R_d111763398e1004" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon discharging to 2&nbsp;V, the K-edge profiles were returned to lower energy and overlapped with the initial state, revealing the reversible redox reaction of Ni and Cu. As for Mn, the valence state is considered to be Mn<sup>4+</sup> at the pristine state and cannot be further oxidized by comparing with the XANES spectra of MnO<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0003">3f</a>). Therefore, the Mn XANES spectra do not show obvious edge shift but only shape change. As the redox reaction of transitional metal elements cannot deliver such high initial charged capacity (139 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, exceeding the theoretical capacity of 125 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> provided by Ni redox), it is reasonably inferred that anionic redox participates into charge compensation upon cycling. As shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8a</a> (Supporting Information), for the pristine electrode, the deconvoluted O 1s peak centered at 529.8&nbsp;eV belongs to the lattice O<sup>2−</sup>, and the additional peaks are assigned to the surface oxygen species (531.5 and 533.5&nbsp;eV), respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0053_R_d111763398e1027" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">53</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0054_R_d111763398e1027" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A new O <i>1s</i> XPS peak emerges at 530.5&nbsp;eV when charged to 4.1&nbsp;V, which can be associated with higher valence state lattice oxygen species O<sub>2</sub><sup>2−</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0021_R_d111763398e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Small.">21</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0023_R_d111763398e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">23</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0029_R_d111763398e1038" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The peak intensity increases as further charging to 4.3&nbsp;V and disappears when discharged to 2&nbsp;V, suggesting the reversible oxygen redox behavior during the charged and discharged process. Combined with in situ differential electrochemical mass spectrometry (DEMS) measurement shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information), it demonstrates that O<sub>2</sub> gas was absent at the first and second cycles of the NZNCMTO as only a small amount of CO<sub>2</sub> evolution was observed, signifying the reversible anionic redox of NZNCMTO upon cycling process.</p>
            
            <p>Fourier Transform (FT) of extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra was conducted to further reveal the TM coordination environment changes at different charge states. As presented in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information), two main peaks located at R ≈1.5 and 2.5 Å corresponds to the TM-O and TM-TM coordination shell, respectively. At 4.3&nbsp;V, both the Ni─O and Ni-TM interaction distance decrease significantly, which can be ascribed oxidation behavior of Ni<sup>2+</sup>/Ni<sup>4+</sup>. The Mn-O interaction distance remains unchanged with only a slight decrease in Mn-TM, while the decreased peaks intensity suggests a more disordered local structure generation. Upon discharging to 2&nbsp;V, the TM-O and TM-TM peaks of Ni and Mn were fully returned to original state, confirming the reversible structure evolution throughout the charged and discharged process. The EXAFS spectrum of Cu reflects a change in its coordination environment, which can be assigned to the high covalency interaction between the Cu 3d and O 2p states.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0024_R_d111763398e1060" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Small.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The above results demonstrate that the unique entropy tuning and dual-site substitution design can greatly enhance the electrochemical reversibility and structural stability of NZNCMTO cathode.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202315437-sec-0070-title">2.5 Theoretical Calculation</h3>
            
            <p>Density functional theory (DFT) calculations were used to gain a deep understanding of the role of introduced electrochemical inert ions and the Na<sup>+</sup> storage behavior of the electrodes. The optimized structure after substitution is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0004">4a</a>. To compare the strength of Mn─O and Ni─O bonds in NNMO and NZNCMTO before and after Cu and Ti co-doping, the crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) method was applied (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0004">4b</a>). It is widely accepted that a larger value of -COHP indicates a stronger TM─O bond. The results from COHP analysis indicate that the introduction of Cu and Ti strengthens the Mn-O (1.0608&nbsp;vs 1.2178&nbsp;eV before and after substitution) and Ni-O (0.4383&nbsp;vs 0.4971&nbsp;eV before and after substitution) bonds, thereby enhancing the overall structural stability of the material. Previous studies<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0043_R_d111763398e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Energy Storage Mater.">43</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0048_R_d111763398e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Chem.">48</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0055_R_d111763398e1086" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - J. Power Sources.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> have also demonstrated that medium-sized Cu and Ti substitution can disrupt the Ni, Mn ordering of the TM layer, effectively reducing TM migration and further enhancing structural stability. Further, the TM-O bond energies in NZNCMTO are shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0004">4c</a>. The introduction of stronger Cu-O bonds (1.8124&nbsp;eV) and Ti-O bonds (2.1835&nbsp;eV) improves the electrochemical cycling stability of the cathode material, which is consistent with the long-cycle test and XRD test results. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0004">4d–g</a> depict the sodium ion migration paths and energy barriers. As is shown in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0004">4g</a>, the energy barriers for Na ions passing through Cu (0.2567&nbsp;eV) and Ti (0.3549&nbsp;eV) are lower compared to passing through Ni/Mn (0.4075&nbsp;eV). Additionally, the density of states (DOS) results (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202315437-fig-0004">4h,i</a>) demonstrate a reduction in the energy band gap from 0.94 to 0.49&nbsp;eV, indicating better electronic conductivity in NZNCMTO. Moreover, Ti substitution leads to increased localization of electrons around oxygen ions in the Ni─, Ti─, and Mn─O bonds, facilitating the charge transfer reaction of oxygen redox. Consequently, NZNCMTO exhibits improved overall performance.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202315437-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/25dcca79-b851-4081-96df-22620bfb81ed/adfm202315437-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>

                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) The optimized structures of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO. b) Comparison of COHP values of Ni-O and Mn-O bonds in NZNCMTO (target) and NNMO (compar.). c) COHP results of entropy-tuned NZNCMTO.                   </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0080">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315437-sec-0080-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, we demonstrate a layered NZNCMTO cathode with superior structural stability and rate capability through an entropy tuning and dual-site substitution complex strategy design. Specifically, owing to the pinning effects of the dopants in both Na and TM slabs, the detrimental structural transformation and oxygen loss are significantly mitigated, leading to superior cycling stability. The incorporation Zn ions in Na slabs expands the interlayered spacing and enables fast Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics. Consequently, the NZNCMTO cathode exhibits superior rate performance (91.54 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 10 C) and enduring cycle stability (nearly 100% capacity retention after 500 cycles at 10 C), surpassing the undoped NNMO cathode material. Combined with DFT calculations, we elucidated that the substitution of Cu and Ti for Ni and Mn engendered stronger covalent bonds with O, thereby enhancing crystal structure and lowering the energy barrier for the migration of sodium ions. The DOS results indicated that the entropy-tuned NZNCMTO manifested enhanced electronic conductivity. These findings underscore the efficacy of the entropy tuning and dual-site substitution strategy as a dependable approach for the systematic design of cathode materials, ensuring proficient ion diffusion rates and structural steadfastness.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0090">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202315437-sec-0090-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0100">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0110">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0120">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315437-sec-0120-title"> Materials synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>[Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub> was prepared using a high-temperature solid-state reaction with the following substrates: A molar ratio of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub> (99.5%, Macklin), ZnO (99%, Macklin), NiO (99%, Macklin), CuO (99%, Macklin), MnO<sub>2</sub> (99%, Macklin), and TiO<sub>2</sub> (99%, Macklin) were mixed into an agate pot with ethanol anhydrous as the dispersant. By ball milling for 48&nbsp;h using a planetary ball mill (QM-3SP2), the mixture was dried at 60&nbsp;°C for 10&nbsp;h. Then, the power was compressed into pellets and calcined at 950&nbsp;°C for 15&nbsp;h. Some control materials such as Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.27</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Mn<sub>0.66</sub>Ti<sub>0.01</sub>O<sub>2</sub>, [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.22</sub>Cu<sub>0.06</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> were synthesized by the same method.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0130">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315437-sec-0130-title"> Electrochemical Measurements</h4>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The electrodes were tested in CR2032 cion cells with the sodium metal as the counter electrodes and Whatman glass fiber filters as separator and 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaClO<sub>4</sub> dissolved in propylene carbonate (PC) with 5 vol.% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) as electrolyte. The positive electrodes were prepared by mixing the active material, super P and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) according to the mass ratio of 8:1:1 with N-methyl-pyrrolidone (NMP) as dispersant and Al foil acts as a fluid collector. Then the prepared electrodes were further dried at 60&nbsp;°C for 12&nbsp;h. The charge-discharge tests of the electrodes were measured on a Neware battery cycler (CT-4008T-5V20 mA-164, Shenzhen, China). Galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT) tests were performed over a voltage range of 2.0–4.3&nbsp;V at a current of 0.1 C for 15&nbsp;min, followed by a 3&nbsp;h rest. Chemical diffusion coefficients were calculated according to the following formula.

                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0140">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315437-sec-0140-title"> Material Characterizations</h4>
                  
                  <p>The XRD patterns of the as-prepared products were collected by X-ray diffraction on Bruker D8 Advance, Germany with Cu Kα radiation at 40&nbsp;kV and 30&nbsp;mA. The elemental ratios of synthesized materials were determined by ICP-OES (Thermo Fisher iCAP PRO). The morphologies of the samples were performed through a field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM, ZEISS, Sigma 500) and (TEM, JEOL, JEM-2100F). HRXPS measurements were performed on an ESCALAB250Xi X-ray photoelectron spectrometer, studying the surface chemical composition and valent state of the element of the products. The differential electrochemical mass spectroscopy (DEMS) experiment was performed with a Swagelok-type cell and a commercial quadrupole mass spectrometer (Hiden Analytical). The X-ray absorption spectroscopy data were collected at 7-BM-B beamline of National Synchrotron Light Source II (NSLS-II) of Brookhaven National Laboratory, in the transmission mode at Ni, Mn, and Cu <i>K</i>-edge.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202315437-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202315437-sec-0150-title"> DFT Calculation Details</h4>
                  
                  <p>All calculations were performed by using the DFT method, as implemented in the Vienna Ab initio Simulation Package (VASP). <sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0056_R_d111763398e1412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">56</a>-<a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0058_R_d111763398e1412" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Phys. Rev. B.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The ion-electron interaction was described by the projector augmented wave (PAW) basis set. In order to more accurately describe the d orbitals of transition metals, the GGA+U method is used. The applied effective U value was given to Ni ions was 6.1&nbsp;eV, to Mn ions was 3.8&nbsp;eV, to Ti ions was 4.2&nbsp;eV, and to Cu ions was 4.0&nbsp;eV.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0059_R_d111763398e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - Phys. Rev. B.">59</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0060_R_d111763398e1417" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - Energy Storage Mater.">60</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The energy cutoff for the plane-wave basis set was 500&nbsp;eV and the force convergence criterion for the relaxation was 0.03&nbsp;eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. The Gamma scheme k-point sampling was used, and k-mesh was denser than 0.04 Å<sup>−1</sup>. The migration of Na ions can be obtained by the method of CI-NEB.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0061_R_d111763398e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - J. Chem. Phys.">61</a>, <a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0062_R_d111763398e1427" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Chem. Phys.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The formation energy of supercell bulk can be obtained by the following formula.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202315437-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202315437-bib-0063_R_d111763398e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - Small.">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>

                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{eqnarray} \Delta E &amp;=&amp;  E\left( {N{a}_xZ{n}_yN{i}_zC{u}_iM{n}_jT{i}_k{O}_l} \right) - xE\left( {N{a}_{bulk}} \right) - yE\left( {Z{n}_{bulk}} \right) - zE\left( {N{i}_{bulk}} \right)\nonumber\\ &amp;&amp; - iE\left( {C{u}_{bulk}} \right) -  jE\left( {M{n}_{bulk}} \right) - kE\left( {T{i}_{bulk}} \right) - \frac{l}{2}E\left( {{O}_2} \right) \end{eqnarray}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(3)</span></div>where <i>E</i>(<i>Na<sub>x</sub>Zn<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Cu<sub>i</sub>Mn<sub>j</sub>Ti<sub>k</sub>O<sub>l</sub></i>) is the energy of <i>Na<sub>x</sub>Zn<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Cu<sub>i</sub>Mn<sub>j</sub>Ti<sub>k</sub>O<sub>l</sub></i>, <i>E</i>(<i>Na<sub>bulk</sub></i>)is derived from the metallic Na crystal, <i>E</i>(<i>Zn<sub>bulk</sub></i>)is calculated from metallic Zn crystal, <i>E</i>(<i>Ti<sub>bulk</sub></i>)is calculated from metallic Ti crystal, <i>E</i>(<i>Ni<sub>bulk</sub></i>) is obtained from metallic Ni crystal, and same thing for <i>E</i>(<i>Cu<sub>bulk</sub></i>) and <i>E</i>(<i>Mn<sub>bulk</sub></i>). And <i>E</i>(<i>O</i><sub>2</sub>) is the energy of dioxygen gas.</div>
                  
                  <div class="paragraph-element">The removing energy of supercell bulk can be obtained by the following formula.

                     <div class="inline-equation" id="adfm202315437-disp-0004"><span class="inline-equation__construct"><!--FIGURE-->
                           <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a862ec71-ac5a-4282-bf65-4b57b5d83e1e/adfm202315437-math-0004.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:1616301X:media:adfm202315437:adfm202315437-math-0004" display="block" location="graphic/adfm202315437-math-0004.png">

                                 <mrow>
                                    <mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi>
                                    <mi>E</mi>

                                    <mi>E</mi>

                                 <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$$\begin{equation}\Delta E\ = \ E\left( {N{a}_xZ{n}_yN{i}_zC{u}_iM{n}_jT{i}_k{O}_l} \right) - E\left( {N{a}_xN{i}_zC{u}_iM{n}_jT{i}_k{O}_l} \right)\ - \ yE\left( {Z{n}_{bulk}} \right)\end{equation}$$</annotation>
                              </semantics></math></span><span class="inline-equation__label">(4)</span></div>
                  </div>
                  
                  <p>where (<i>Na<sub>x</sub>Zn<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Cu<sub>i</sub>Mn<sub>j</sub>Ti<sub>k</sub>O<sub>l</sub></i>) is the energy of <i>Na<sub>x</sub>Zn<sub>y</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Cu<sub>i</sub>Mn<sub>j</sub>Ti<sub>k</sub>O<sub>l</sub></i>, <i>E</i>(<i>Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Cu<sub>i</sub>Mn<sub>j</sub>Ti<sub>k</sub>O<sub>l</sub></i>) is the energy of <i>Na<sub>x</sub>Ni<sub>z</sub>Cu<sub>i</sub>Mn<sub>j</sub>Ti<sub>k</sub>O<sub>l</sub></i>, and <i>E</i>(<i>Zn<sub>bulk</sub></i>)is calculated from metallic Zn crystal.</p>
